0446541478|4.0|Dan Senor and Saul Singer are not great story-tellers, but they do have a great story -- the rise of Israel's innovation economy.  It's a fascinating tale of a culture that emphasizes generalists who are adept and agile problem-solvers.  The authors try to break down the formula of Israel's success -- society's flat hierarchy (where everybody knows each other), decentralization (everyone is supposed to make his own decisions and judgements), internationalization (Israelis are avid travellers, and Israel has a mighty disapora), its military service culture, and a hands-off government -- but I think that there's two compelling reasons why Israel has succeeded:  Its culture of innovation and experimentation, and a unified society with a higher sense of purpose than just material attainment.    The authors are a bit too glib about Israel's success, and it can be annoying at times.  But overall it's a great introduction to Israel's innovation economy.
B004BJMEO6|5.0|Elena Undone is by far, the best lesbian movie I've ever seen. The chemistry between the two women is palpable, the script is well written and believable, and the acting is wonderful by the two leading actresses as well as the supporting cast. Traci Dinwiddie (Peyton) especially stands out when she is preparing to share her feelings with her up-til-then platonic friend. Her discomfort yet innocent naivety was delightful. She conveys with one raised eyebrow or sigh, her embarrassed, self consciousness. There were two other scenes where she took my breath away: in the short love scene when her lover, Elena (Necar Zadegan), asserts herself sexually and Peyton is forced to face her own control & trust issues, fears, and vulnerability; and in the swimming pool when her pain overwhelms her after their breakup. Peyton's pain becomes our pain as she disappears sobbing underneath the shimmering water.<br /><br />I actually regard The Longest Kiss as the first love scene in Elena Undone. The energy and determination with which Elena bursts into the room would cause any heart to race and the poignant dialogue between kisses rang so true that it only added to their rising passion. Necar Zadegan is enchanting as she wrestles with her new found reality of finding herself in love with a woman. She owns the screen in the morning-after breakfast scene and in the park when her dreams begin to crumble as Peyton emotionally steps back and pushes her away. Two other actresses deserve special mention: Mary Wells as Wave, Peyton's best friend, was fabulous! She's a best friend everyone would want. And Sabrina Fuster as Tori was simply charming. They both offered sterling performances.<br /><br />One final mention...the music is remarkable. Between Jennifer Corday's \\"Make Me Come Undone\\", Marc Chait's score, and Sabrina Fuster's \\"Suddenly\\", the music pulls at our heartstrings and keeps us emotionally tuned in every moment.<br /><br />In closing, I don't know how Nicole Conn does it. A great script is the key and this was indeed a wonderful, reality based script. The movie just gets better and better. It's important to note too that Nicole Conn has given us more than just a lesbian movie. It's a movie about relationships and an affirmation that if we pay close attention and truly show up in our lives, then our chances of finding love is better than we think.<br /><br />Janet Liss

0451232577|2.0|Fall of Giants is supposed to be Part 1 of a trilogy.  Unfortunately, that conceit governs the book.  I read it because I am interested in World War I and its effects on subsequent history.  Fall of Giants borrows its form from Tolstoy's War and Peace.  We follow significant families, supposedly typical of their nation, class, and age as they experience the events at the beginning of the 20th century. We have a Brit of the upper class, a coalminer Welshman, an American diplomat, a Russian and his brother, and a German family.  Notice anything missing from this pageant?  The French are barely mentioned.  How do you write a book about World War I and just use France as if it is the chessboard on which the action occurred? Even if you were just setting up a World War 2 sequel, the effects of both World Wars on France were profound. A great deal of Fall of Giants is spent on the details of the Russian Revolution, a significant event, but obviously setting up Volumes 2 and 3.  Fall of Giants is verbose.  If you want to read about World War I, you'll learn a lot more from the slender volume of All Quiet on the Western Front or Barbara Tuchman's books than from the 800+ pages of Follett's book.

B0031YJFCQ|2.0|I was very excited about the first book. Although not particularly impressed by the writing style of Larsson I was truly amazed by interesting and extremely engaging story line. Simply Excellent!!!<br /><br />Second book was less exciting but it retained certain attractive qualities: intriguing background stories, dynamic plot line and almost encyclopedic insights. I was a bit alarmed by more apparent division of characters to black and white. The main group started to become more and more static, they stopped evolving - all the good guys extremely good in every way, all the bad guys are just monsters. Borderline supernatural abilities of some of the main characters also raised an eyebrow but I brushed it off as an artistic decision of an eccentric author.<br /><br />And then I got to the third book. I was terrified by how self righteous and uninteresting all the main characters have became. Blomkvist turned into a conflicting crossbreed between a ladies-man who will pretty much sleep with anything that moves and \\"mother Teresa\\" who is soooo sensitive and understanding of all the problems of every woman he meets that he just can't help but have sex with them all. To summarize he evolved from a strong yet caring male into a whiny douche-bag.<br />I also noted that there was not one negative female character in the third book. It was an absolutely absurd setting. In a way instead of patriarchal system reader was offered a polar reverse with matriarchal one. Don't get me wrong I am all pro woman's rights and I agree that there are great injustices committed against women socially as well as in the work place but it turns into a very one sided and therefore incredibly boring nag-fest. In light of this quality the third novel reads like a self-righteous rant in the style of Ayn Rand.<br /><br />Another interesting contradiction is the double standard when discussing the social contract. On one hand Blomkvist/Larsson constantly wags his finger at any and all figures of authority, constantly lecturing everyone how they should abide by the rules while cherishing and upholding the democratic structure of society and constitution. But on the other hand it seems when the opportunity is right the law can certainly be taking into the hands of vigilantes, no matter how unlawful the activity is, as long as it coincides with what Blomkvist/Larsson ideas of what is good and moral. I'm not awfully familiar with Swedish justice system but it seems the main character commits numerous and pretty grievous accounts of abstraction of justice for the sake of sensationalism and profit. In that sense journalism becomes a sacred calf. It is ironic that the same type of cross examination of the absurd self governing and self justifying principles, on which bases Peter Teleborian finds Lisbeth crazy, can be applied to Larssons own ideology.<br /><br />To summarize the trilogy:<br /><br />1-Fantastic<br />2-Bearable<br />3-Waste of paper<br /><br />Given the degradation of the style of the trilogy I would only recommend the first book, well maybe the second one too. B00026WU4G|4.0|\\"Infidels\\" holds quite an interesting place in Dylan's canon, in that it's one record where what didn't make the record had a profound impact on its overall quality and place in his catalog. While almost every Dylan album has a good amount of quality outtakes that should've seen the light of day sooner than they did (if at all), \\"Infidels\\" had such blindingly superior songs to some of what actually made the album. What was going through Dylan's mind when he passed off \\"Blind Willie McTell,\\" \\"Foot of Pride,\\" and \\"Someone's Got a Hold of My Heart\\" for duds like \\"Sweetheart Like You,\\" \\"Neighborhood Bully,\\" and \\"Union Sundown\\" is a mystery for the ages. Had Dylan included these gems, \\"Infidels\\" would've easily been his best work of the 1980s' without a second thought. As it stands, Dylan had to wait until 1989's \\"Oh Mercy\\" until he made a record that truly stood up to his earlier accomplishments.<br /><br />That said, despite these errors, \\"Infidels\\" is a remarkably solid album: the good of the album is REALLY good, and often great. The opening \\"Jokerman\\" sails along on a reggae groove as Dylan attacks false prophets with a venom that escalates with each verse. \\"License to Kill\\" has some admittedly ridiculous lyrics (\\"Man has invented his doom / the first step was touching the moon...\\") but also has enough respectable insights and truisms in it to make it work. \\"Man of Peace\\" has Dylan dipping back into the fire and brimstone well of \\"Slow Train Coming\\" with a warning of Satan's return to Earth and not to always trust those with good intentions. The lyrics are incredibly clever and the groove is incredibly infectious. \\"I and I\\" is a dark, brooding reggae number with some of Dylan's most cryptic and inscrutable lyrics. Finally, the closing \\"Don't Fall Apart on Me Tonight\\" is a charmingly desperate plea for companionship with a smooth, sexy Dylan vocal that ends the record in style.<br /><br />Dylan reunited with Mark Knopfler for the first time since \\"Slow Train Coming,\\" and they strike gold a second time here: Knopfler manages to make the record sound slick and polished without smothering its edge and grit, and his guitar playing is infectious and flavorful. \\"Infidels\\" is not quite the masterpiece it could've been, but is still a very solid entry into the Dylan catalog.d a second time here: Knopfler manages to make the record sound slick and polished without smothering its edge and grit, and his guitar playing is infectious and flavorful. \\"Infidels\\" is not quite the masterpiece it could've been, but is still a very solid entry into the Dylan catalog.

0393338827|4.0|This is going to be less a 'book review' than a brief commentary.   In providing the former, this: 'The Big Short' is a amicably-written explanation of The Great Financial Meltdown that despite being a gripping tale, is still confounding to both the brain...and the spirit. This is the second book by Mr. Lewis I've read on this theme...the first being 'Boomerang'...and though I believe I understand more of 'what happened' back then, I'm still bewildered, and the questions I have are less about the specifics, or even 'Why was it allowed to happen?' than issues a few steps back from all that. Nevertheless, my gratitude to the author for once more adding some delineation to the subject.   In dealing with the latter...   I'm not going to say much that's new here, but I'll proceed anyway: I believe that one of the greatest bulwarks against 'progress' towards a more humane society in North America is the conflation going on regarding 'capitalism' and 'democracy'.  People see the word 'capitalism' and think 'democracy'.   And in the US especially, you don't mess with anyone's democracy...and therefore, you don't mess with anyone's capitalism. No matter how much each need to be 'messed with'.   Moreover, in America once you wrap an issue in the flag, all discussion effectively ends. So it's very difficult to talk about economics, about 'the travails of the 99%', about political reform, about just about anything having to do with Life Being Lived, without setting off a conflagration. Because, of course, it's all connected to democracy, the imperfect experiment, but the only system that 'In God We Trust' Americans have any faith in. Therefore, nothing really changes.   Add into the mix the utter manipulation of modern media to effect the whims and priorities by Those People From Both Arenas That Dicate The Dance (capitalism and democracy) as well as the pretty-shameful ignorance on the part of the average participant of each about each arena...and you've got the mess we're witnessing today.   People will not fight for something, notion, a cause, that they don't grasp. And in both instances, capitalism and democracy, there's been a pretty much wholesale 'hands-off' attitude about them...resulting in, for example, nothing being done about What Happened On Wall Street: nobody was charged.   What did I most get from 'The Big Short'? The inkling that if what happened had happened in another arena...say, the manufacturing of biological weapons, for instance...and there had been a catastrophe of equal proportions, then all Hell would have broken loose and that previous landscape would never have been the same. People simply would not have tolerated it. But because we're talking 'capitalism', because there's so little comprehension about it, because there's so much resignation and apathy...combined with 'capitalism' being the great dance partner with 'democracy'...nothing was done.   (A film adaptation of Mr. Lewis' book...the third one to be done, after 'Moneyball' and 'The Blind Side'...is schedded for release in 2014.)  Personal rating: 9/10

I just think it would have been a better story if it didn't try so hard to be sure it stepped on each and every base on its way around the story.<br /><br />Cinderella is always a second-class citizen. In Meyer's variation, Cinder is second-class because she is a cyborg. Cyborgs are considered less-than-human by those who have been fortunate enough not to have lived through catastrophic accidents such as the one that cost Cinder her hand and her foot at age 11.<br /><br />But Cinder does not remember the traumatic accident, or anything about her early life. And the man who might have told her is dead. Linh Garan adopted her and left her in the care of his wife Adra just before his death. Garan was an inventor; he liked to tinker with things. He may have adopted Cinder to tinker with. He might have done something to her internal processes. But no one knows, least of all Cinder.<br /><br />Adra hates and resents Cinder, while at the same time greedily taking every credit that Cinder earns as a gifted mechanic. Adra is entitled to retain all of Cinder's earnings, forever. Adra is Cinder's guardian, and Cinder's earnings are the only thing keeping the household out of the poorhouse. The household, of course, contains not only the nasty stepmother Adra, but Cinder's stepsisters, Peony and Pearl.<br /><br />This Cinderella tale has been transplanted in time and place. We are still on Earth, but it is a future, post-apocalyptic Earth, after a dreadful Fourth World War first devastated, then finally united humanity under an Imperial Commonwealth. Cinder lives in the Eastern Commonwealth capital of New Beijing. The moon was not just settled, but broke away from the Earth pre-WWIV and created its own government. The Lunars have become not just a separate government, but in some ways, a separate race, because they have the capacity to manipulate bio-electric energy to a point that seems like magic. It's a LOT like the Force in Star Wars, and too many of the Lunars mostly act like the Sith. The Lunar Queen and Emperor Palpatine would probably have a lot in common, if they didn't try to kill each other on sight.<br /><br />Prince Kai brings his personal android to Cinder at her stall in the market to repair. He says it's because it was his teaching android when he was growing up, and he's emotionally attached to it.<br /><br />Cinder, who shouldn't have the neural circuitry to swoon, practically swoons over Prince Kai. She is able to suppress her reaction. What she isn't able to suppress is her knowledge that he is lying. There is something important about the little android, and it isn't merely an emotional attachment.<br /><br />Kai lets something slip, he is doing research on leutmosis, the deadly plague that is sweeping the world. It is 100% fatal. Cinder wonders if his android contains some of his research.<br /><br />The research that Kai might or might not be conducting becomes even more important to Cinder when her stepsister Peony contracts the deadly plague. Peony was the only person who truly cared for Cinder, and now she is gone. And in her rage, Adra signs Cinder over the government as a test subject. Cinder, as a cyborg, has no rights at all.<br /><br />Once Cinder is tested, the truth of her origins begins to be revealed, not to Cinder, but to others who have been searching for her desperately. Nothing in her life has been as it has seemed.<br /><br />But Cinder is going to the ball.<br /><br />Escape Rating B-: The cover of this book is awesome. The book has its moments but I figured out the big reveal very, very early on. It's better if the surprise remains a surprise as long as possible. Instead, everything was telegraphed miles ahead of time.<br /><br />I loved the scene where Cinder drives to the ball and shows up in all her grease-stained glory to try to rescue Kai, but I saw it coming miles away.<br /><br />And, as many other reviewers have noted, what does Kai look like? He's never described. Ever. There's a rule somewhere that all brides are, by definition, beautiful. Is there a corollary that all princes are handsome? Therefore there's no requirement that they be described? Is he blond? Does he have black hair? Brown eyes? Blue eyes? Swoon-worthy is just not a sufficient description.<br /><br />And I still want to find out what happens next. I want to see that Lunar Queen get what she deserves. 1451627289|5.0|I read the NYT book review for this story, and thank goodness I did, because I don't normally pay much attention to Stephen King's novels - I think he's a good storyteller, but am not so fond of being frightened.  It was apparent from the review that this book was more fantasy than horror (which is true), and so I decided to give it a try.  I read the book's 800+ pages like I was reading a comic book - the writing is so visual and the storylines are so compelling that it was all I could do to make myself put it down when my eyes got tired.  But don't be fooled that this is just a story about time travel, or the innocence/guilt of Lee Harvey Oswald: it's also a love story, a story of morality and spirituality, and a cautionary tale about what happens if we play God and mess with the order of things as they've happened so far.  I was moved by it and still cannot stop thinking about it.  I'm certain the movie rights have already been snapped up, but this is one of those rare books that - despite the tremendous film potential - will be more vivid and interesting having read it than seeing it on screen.

B005IQXUQW|5.0|This is a simply fantastic box of all six volumes of Caf&eacute; Zimmermann's recordings of Bach's \\"Concerts avec plusiers instruments.\\" These include all six Brandenburg Concertos, the four Overtures or Orchestral Suites and the concertos for violin, oboe and harpsichords in various combinations. I have listed the works below. The music is all magnificent and played with a freshness combined with depth of understanding which is a real joy.<br /><br />Caf&eacute; Zimmermann are a small ensemble, generally playing with one instrument to a part and this, combined with terrific musicianship and scholarship, gives their interpretations a real zing in the livelier movements and genuine beauty and emotion in the slower passages. To me, this is what Bach should sound like: sometimes dancing, sometimes deeply spiritual, sometimes simply lovely. I have a much-loved set of Bach concertos by Trevor Pinnock and while these won't replace them in my affections, they will certainly stand alongside them and be played at least as often.<br /><br />The other great strength of these discs is the programming. Many sets of these concertos group, say, the harpsichord concertos or the violin concertos together, and I find that I often don't want to listen to all of them one after another. Here, each disc has one Brandenburg Concerto, for example, and a varied combination of other concertos, so that playing the whole disc is a varied pleasure and I am always happy to let a whole disc play rather than changing it after one or two concertos.<br /><br />In short, this box - the fruits of ten years' work - is an unalloyed delight and guaranteed to give immense pleasure for many years. 0451529065|2.0|Though my initial motive to read Darwin's \\"Origin of the Species\\" was most casual, as I started reading it I grew respectful of Charles Darwin in many ways. He was wisely conscious of distinguishing actual instances from supposition, inferences and conjecture, something near to nil in anything about evolution by contemporary authors. In books from Dawkins and most moderns, facts and guesswork are rather recklessly blended so that the reader becomes mislead or even deceived by their writings. Seeking out, I notice every factual instance Darwin presented was always within the species whereas everything beyond the species was supposition, inferences, conjecture or \\"extrapolation\\" as it goes to today. Evolution has no instances of animals (or plants) growing into a whole new family or genera, thus one very valid reason to not be so convinced that this can happen.<br /><br />Darwin does (quite honestly) continuously support his theory with \\"ignorance\\"; \\"But it deserves especial notice that the more important objections relate to questions on which we are confessedly ignorant; nor do we know how ignorant we are.\\"...\\"no one ought to feel surprise at much remaining as yet unexplained on the origin of species, if we make due allowance for our profound ignorance\\"... This is only a very small sample of how Darwin regularly relies on \\"ignorance\\" to excuse explanations, and I give him much credit for the honesty he displays. But it's the intent of science to use knowledge to endorse theories, not ignorance or lack of knowledge. Sadly, even to today, we honestly, really haven't gained enough enlightenment to rid \\"ignorance\\" from evolution.<br /><br />Right from the start, even Darwin assigns power and force to \\"Natural Selection\\", but he (as no one does to today) never makes any attempt to explain that power or force, other than it is blind and unpurposeful. Where does this power and force come from? What causes it to guide and do what it does? How can it, for example, cause certain fish, bugs and mushrooms to grow light emitting organs with no awareness of what light, organs or even perception is?  Thus a very valid reason to not be so convinced of the \\"powers\\" of \\"Natural Selection.\\"<br /><br />Darwin writes (correctly) throughout his book on how certain organs or parts can be lost due to non-use. The eyes of fish that live in caves or moles and burrowing rodents, wings of beetles that have no need to fly, and yet perhaps generations later \\"reversions to long lost characters occur.\\" (We now know DNA plays a roll in such  \\"characters\\" being lost and later growing back; the Italian wall lizards introduced to Croatia grew parts that were lost, yet its ancestors and other lizards still have them, being a classic case). Darwin writes quite a list and plenty of facts and instances on parts and organs in  the process of growing useless (vestigial) but has absolutely no instances of new parts and organs in the process of growing (and there are no new parts to this day) thus a very valid reason to not be so convinced that this can happen.<br /><br />It appears many creationists back in the 1850's insisted God created each and every species for each and every location, or at least, this is what Darwin portrays them to believe. His arguments (rightfully) disprove this, but never come close to disproving the Biblical Creation account showing God created \\"kinds\\"  (Genesis 1:11-25) that grew into the many varieties we have today. All is fine there.<br /><br />I'm saddened on how many people that believe in evolution have never read their \\"Darwinian bible.\\" Darwin's accuracy and honesty is an example few authors of evolution today emulate.

I had the opinion like many others, this was about an idealistic young man that was living through romantic conceptions and meets an untimely end when reality is introduced.<br /><br />Krakauer does a nice job of tracing McCandless's life and the motivations that drove him into the wild. While McCandless is a youthful idealist, many demons of his upbringing drove him as well. Unfortunately, before age can temper his expectations and prepare him for life's challenges, his missteps are fatal.<br /><br />While the book kept me interested, there were times it got long-winded with long quotes from friends and family. I was about to put the book down when it really hit the strongest part.<br /><br />Krakauer writes about his own experience that parallels McCandless's, how his angst took him to a formidable mountain rather than Alaska. How he survived and, to his good fortune, learned from his experience.<br /><br />Finally, Krakauer methodically traces McCandless's footsteps to the bus in the wild and, very convincingly, determines McCandless was not as ill-prepared and foolish as some suppose. His death was the result of many things, and Krakauer makes a strong argument for what might have been what really brought about McCandless's death.

0062082353|3.0|Ive read many many books about Spec Ops and have visited the Navy Amphib Base in Coronado to observe BUDS training with a JAG friend.  My respect for the Teams runs deep, including for the author Chief Kyle.<br /><br />I cannot say the same for his book.  He was trained as a skilled marksman and not as a writer, and it shows.  The prosaic recollection is written at the level of a high school composition which style goes dry at times, even while describing hair-raising events.  The reader's mind seeks out more details, more context because the situations can be quite gripping, yet the storytelling leaves something to be desired.  It also featured editorial shortcomings for which his publishing team should hang their heads, including misspelling (good lord!) and repetitive explanation of certain military lexicon.  If only Chief Kyle's literary skill matched his bravery and the unbelievable sights sounds smells acts he witnessed (and performed) ...<br /><br />Some will be troubled by Chief Kyle characterizing Iraqi combatants as savages.  While he portrays himself as a dispassionate professional merely executing his patriotic duty by lining up tangos in his scope, the reader easily infers that he privately relished and enjoyed killing Iraqis.  True, combat is \\"kill or you die\\" circumstances.  But cmon, savages?  Really?  That may have been the psychological/religious bargain he had to strike to be able to perform his job, and to do it so well, but its still difficult to read such a declaration.<br /><br />On the plus side, the book centers around his deployments and training cycles and in the field experiences.  Thankfully he avoids giving yet another trite summary of BUDs or AOT.  At times funny, frequently self deprecating, and always cocky/confident, it was an interesting enough read for a weekend.  Not the most high-brow work, but keeps the reader entertained enough to finish it.<br /><br />I am very glad Chief Kyle made it home to his family and was able to share his valuable perspective.  He has no doubt earned every right to freely comment, and then some.  However biased, he shared an important perspective that needs to be understood, even by those who may not agree with it. B000000QFV|5.0|A quick note on how I came to discover TYR in early 2012.  For years, I only listened to Ozzy-fronted Sabbath, and would have nothing to do with any other version of the band.  Finally, after the superb album, The Devil You Know, by the Mob Rules line-up, I got into the Dio albums, which are nothing short of amazing.  From there, I checked into the Tony Martin era.  I actually had Headless Cross on cassette way back when because it got pretty regular airplay on Headbanger's Ball, so I already knew that album was OK even though I wasn't into it much back then.  I got Eternal Idol and was blown away.  Martin's debut with the band sounded nearly as potent as any of the Dio albums, and certainly worthy of much more attention than it gets.  So after wearing out EI, then picking up Headless Cross on CD to re-appreciate that album, it was on to TYR.<br /><br />Wow, I wish I'd known enough to get this back in 1990.  What an incredible, ridiculously unknown entry in the Sabbath catalog!  I have yet to get Cross Purposes or Forbidden, but for my money, this is the best Tony Martin album of the first three.  The sound is pure Sabbath, with guitars reminiscent of the late 70's-early 80's incarnations of the band.  A couple of tracks, like The Law Maker (which is AWESOME!) and Valhalla boast guitars as heavy as anything in Sabbath's history.  The Law Maker almost has a speed metal vibe, with classic Iommi riffing that sounds reminiscent of the Ozzy era, being driven by a pummeling beat from Cozy Powell.  Valhalla is another stomping, heavy song that stands up to just about anything from the band's past.  The whole album has a heavier vibe than both Eternal Idol and Headless Cross, with more energetic riffs and beats in songs like Anno Mundi, Jerusalem, and The Sabbath Stones.  Feels Good to Me, despite being a slower track written for radio, features parts a couple minutes in that have the epic guitar sound heard on some of the solo sections of Heaven and Hell, while the main riff to album closer Heaven in Black recalls Children of the Grave.  I really didn't find anything unlikable on this album.<br /><br />No matter what era of Black Sabbath you're talking about, Tony Iommi has always been an incredible songwriter, and is finally getting some long overdue credit among heavy metal fans for some of those albums like TYR that got bashed back in the 80's and 90's because of their \\"outdated\\" sound, or because of Ozzy's far wider success during that time.  As of this review, the original line-up is set to release a new album this fall after a summer of touring.  I'm completely excited and can't wait to hear how they sound, and I hope the success they're bound to have reuniting with Ozzy will convince even more people to search out the less-known Sabbath titles and enjoy what many missed out on in years past.  Tony didn't forget how to play guitar after Ozzy's departure, or for that matter, Geezer's or Bill's either.  Whoever the singer is, just about any Black Sabbath album is good for a solid eight or nine songs of classic heavy metal, as I've come to learn over the last few years.  If you're not steadfastly against Black Sabbath without Ozzy or Dio (or even if you are), definitely check out TYR, Eternal Idol, and some of the other previously disdained titles.  You'll be surprised.m closer Heaven in Black recalls Children of the Grave.  I really didn't find anything unlikable on this album. 031286504X|5.0|I AM LEGEND - which, by the way, is a ridiculously awesome title - is Richard Matheson's most celebrated story. I AM LEGEND, first published in 1954, is a staple in the genre of horror literature, a haunting novella that inspired three feature film adaptations. Three feature films which still don't do justice to the book. Matheson's game-changing scientific approach to vampirism, combined with his impeccable storytelling skills, influenced a host of renowned horror writers such as Stephen King and Brian Lumley. Matheson chronicles the harrowing odyssey of one man's struggle to survive in a blighted world. We track three years in the life of Robert Neville. In describing that three-year span, \\"bleak\\" would be understating it.<br /><br />The vampire virus struck without warning; it rapidly consumed the world, infected or killed off the entire human population but one. And now Robert Neville finds himself the last remaining human, clawing out a meager existence. By daylight, Neville stocks up on necessities and hunts down the vampires. By night, he barricades himself in his fortified home while the revenants roam the landscape and taunt him on his doorstep. It's only natural that Robert Neville finds himself quietly going insane. Matheson has never been more pronounced in his ability to convey a man's unendurable loneliness and sense of alienation. I AM LEGEND is haunting and frightening and addictive. I can see why it's regarded as an absolute classic, science and the supernatural and nerve-jangling human drama relentlessly melded into a nightmarish read. The pages almost turn themselves.<br /><br />I AM LEGEND, understandably, is the featured content in this anthology, but don't dismiss the other stories, which range from potent three-page drive-bys to more-fleshed out blood-curdlers that run over twenty pages. In this anthology, Matheson weaves a thread that seems to suggest that sinister, unfathomable forces, while not exactly always aligned against us, nevertheless inhabit our daily existence. While one or two of these tales is weaker than others - I found \\"Dance of the Dead\\" an offputting read, mostly because of the jarring style Matheson adopts - he manages to consistently display his arsenal of black humor (\\"The Near Departed,\\" \\"The Funeral\\"), his knack for psychological suspense (\\"Mad House\\"), and twisted imagination. I love his penchant for throwing in the occasional punchline or nasty swerve. \\"Dress of White Silk,\\" \\"Mad House,\\" and \\"Person to Person\\" are particularly noteworthy \\"What the F---?!\\" stories.<br /><br />I AM LEGEND kicks off the anthology; here's the rest:<br /><br />- \\"Buried Talents\\" (1987) - In a fairground, a man with infallible aim approaches a game booth. It would've better had the booth runner not effed with him.<br /><br />- \\"The Near Departed\\" (1987) - A pale little man enters a mortician's office and makes arrangements for his deceased wife. But there's a twist.<br /><br />- \\"Prey\\" (1969) - A long-suffering daughter inadvertently lets loose a murderous Zuni fetish doll who has this sharp little knife.<br /><br />- \\"Witch War\\" (1951) - Seven chattery little girls are conscripted into some unnamed war.<br /><br />- \\"Dance of the Dead\\" (1954) - In the aftermath of a war in which some of the dead are resurrected, four kids drive down to St. Louis to see a zombie stage act.<br /><br />- \\"Dress of White Silk\\" (1951) - The moral of this one is you shouldn't make fun of disturbed little girls' mothers or their mothers' dresses.<br /><br />- \\"Mad House\\" (1952) - Psychobolie phenomena: the paranormal link between the animate and inanimate. Matheson submits a story in which a habitually frustrated writer's unchecked anger, when released, doesn't dissipate but instead is channeled into the inanimate objects in his home. Sucks for that writer.<br /><br />- \\"The Funeral\\" (1955) - How Morton Silkline became the funeral director of choice for the supernatural community.<br /><br />- \\"From Shadowed Places\\" (1960)- A tale of darkest juju and prejudice and how primal forces roil beneath even the most professional of demeanors. It's Matheson's cautionary way of advising his readers to be kind to starving witch doctors.<br /><br />- \\"Person to Person\\" (1989) - From 3am to 6am, Millman hears a phone ringing... in his head. That is never a good thing.- A tale of darkest juju and prejudice and how primal forces roil beneath even the most professional of demeanors. It's Matheson's cautionary way of advising his readers to be kind to starving witch doctors.<br /><br />- \\"Person to Person\\" (1989) - From 3am to 6am, Millman hears a phone ringing... in his head. That is never a good thing.

SPOILERS UNTIL FURTHER NOTICE  Everything I liked about the Maze Runner and the Scorch Trials was absent from this novel. As a reader I grew accustomed to the ever present mysteries in the novel. The answers to mysteries about why WICKED exists and why all those kids were trapped in a maze were quickly and unceremoniously answered. Dashner proceeds to create a semi-unnecessary sub-mystery in the form of a note with hidden contents, given to our protagonist by one of his fellow Gladers. I genuinely thought that the note would be something stunning and revelatory...nope. It was a request, and one that I don't even think was necessary, given the way Thomas actually responded to the contents of the note. I think it would have been more effective to take Thomas to the side and say, \\"Bro, if I'm about to go, just kill me. If you don't, I'll probs try to kill you and you'll have to do it anyway. For real, I was a one dimensional character and we weren't even THAT close, when you think about it.\\"  And then, the nonstop action and go go go? It was fine, but it just seemed excessivefor a trilogy that deals with introspection and the troubles of forced waiting and uncertainty. The characters have been at the whim of others for hundreds of pages, and for some reason they seemed foreign when they were forced to be assertive and to make their own decisions.  And while this may be an excessively picky critique, I have to say it: I have never witnessed a more boring love triangle in my entire life, and I sat through Win a Date with Tad Hamilton. Did he really like either girl? This romance was so forced. And not appealing in the slightest. If Dashner didn't want to bother with a romance, he didn't need to; the trilogy would have been fine without it.  END SPOILERS  Bottom line, we finally get some answers. Some. Any fan of the Maze Runner needs to read this book, just for some closure. It'll leave you wanting more, but it's necessary. It's like the dessert course at the end of a five course meal--you're not hungry, your stomach hurts, and it doesn't even taste good, BUT you just keep eating because you're not a quitter and you've made it this far.

0312611692|3.0|This is admirable but uneven attempt to sketch out a sort of universal history.  Morris goes well beyond the question of his title to produce an overview of Eurasian, and to a lesser extent, world history, over the last few thousand years.  Morris takes human nature as essentially fixed and universal with no significant ethnic differences.  He then focuses on how civilizations developed at the two approximate poles of the Eurasian continent.  The latter is his focus because civilizations emerged first in these regions and enjoyed a de facto technological head start and persistent advantages over other areas that developed civilizations, such as the Western Hemisphere or Africa.  Morris provides a series of reasonably good overviews of the development of civilizations in Western Eurasia and East Asia.  Following the suggestion of Jared Diamond, this is a biogeographical story with the availability of plants and animals suitable for domestication leading to the emergence of agriculture, more sedentary societies, and civilizations.  Morris then follows the trajectories of the Western Core (the ancient Near East and its successors) and the Eastern Core (China) over subsequent centuries.  This is a story of irregular progress and decline with the Eastern and Western Cores outpacing each other at different times.  A distinctive feature of this analysis is that Morris attempts to go beyond impressionistic analysis.  He constructs an index of civilizational achievements which he uses as a yardstick to compare the Eastern and Western Cores.  One can argue about the construction and use of his scale but this is an interesting and useful attempt.<br /><br />Morris does an overall good job with his narrative, summarizing a large amount of secondary literature.  He shows the rise and falls of major Eastern and Western societies, how they interacted via trade across Eurasia, and also looks into what determines the rise and fall of major polities and societies.  Climactic change is argued to be particularly important.  Despite the collapse of some major polities, like the Roman Empire, there is overall upward progress on Morris' scale across centuries.  The Eastern and Western Cores are at approximate parity until the Industrial Revolution.  Morris argues that the Eastern Core is now catching up and may surpass the Western Core.<br /><br />Despite the good narrative and efforts at scientific classification, there are some real problems with Morris' analysis.  His definition of the Western Core is essentially that of cultural continuity.  The Western Core migrates from the ancient Near East to the Mediterranean to Europe and then the borders of the North Atlantic.  In contrast, the Eastern Core is basically defined geographically as China.  But how much cultural continuity is there in modern China?  The latter is a nation-state, its government involves a form of mass politics, it pursues a form of industrial capitalism, and its intellectual culture is dominated by the natural sciences.  The Chinese have adopted the package of major cultural innovations developed in Western Europe and North America around the Industrial Revolution, a marked discontinuity with its past.  The present comparison isn't then between East or West, its properly a comparison between different versions of the Western Core.<br /><br />Morris also has a rather deterministic, monocausal view of important events, particularly the genesis of the Industrial Revolution.  He sees this as being driven by geography.  Because Europe had easier access across the Atlantic to the Western Hemisphere, it developed an Industrial Revolution.  While the \\"ecological windfall\\" of the Americas (and the corresponding availability of coerced labor from Africa) is probably a crucial factor in the genesis of the Industrial Revolution, this kind of monocausal explanation is unlikely to correct.  Morris is determined to avoid what he calls \\"lock in\\" explanations of more recent events.  In particular, he attacks the idea that some unique feature of Greek culture set the West on the inevitable road to the modern world.  While I agree with his desire to avoid this kind of determinism, there is some throwing out the baby with the bathwater.  Our natural sciences originate with Hellenic and Hellenistic science and math.  While there is a long and torturous path from Greece to the 17th century Scientific Revolution, Greek science and math (transmitted to Europe and elaborated by great Muslim intellectuals) provide the essential germ of our scientific tradition.  No Western science, no Industrial Revolution.  There is little evidence that Chinese civilization ever developed something analogous to the Western scientific tradition (see the final third of Mark Elvin's interesting Retreat of the Elephants).  Morris greatly underestimates the complex genesis and deep roots of the Industrial Revolution.<br /><br />The final section of the book, some speculation on the future, is the weakest.  Morris seems taken with the improbable ideas of Raymond Kurzweil about the so-called Singularity and also seems to have an exaggerated concept of the impact of biotechnology. He also seems to have an exaggerated idea of the likelihood of an acute collapse of civilization.  In fact, what we're likely faced with the continued articulation of our industrial civilization and the countervailing consequences of gradual environmental degradation caused by our impressive technology.that some unique feature of Greek culture set the West on the inevitable road to the modern world.  While I agree with his desire to avoid this kind of determinism, there is some throwing out the baby with the bathwater. While there is a long and torturous path from Greece to the 17th century Scientific Revolution, Greek science and math (transmitted to Europe and elaborated by great Muslim intellectuals) provide the essential germ of our scientific tradition.  No Western science, no Industrial Revolution.  There is little evidence that Chinese civilization ever developed something analogous to the Western scientific tradition (see the final third of Mark Elvin's interesting Retreat of the Elephants).  Morris greatly underestimates the complex genesis and deep roots of the Industrial Revolution.    The final section of the book, some speculation on the future, is the weakest.  Morris seems taken with the improbable ideas of Raymond Kurzweil about the so-called Singularity and also seems to have an exaggerated concept of the impact of biotechnology. He also seems to have an exaggerated idea of the likelihood of an acute collapse of civilization.  In fact, what we're likely faced with the continued articulation of our industrial civilization and the countervailing consequences of gradual environmental degradation caused by our impressive technology.

Find Your Own Story  It is vital to have a compelling story to rally customers, generate word-of-mouth (mouse) and generate press buzz. Not sitting on the sidelines but being in the arena. The book says that there are only 6 steps but I consider the final chapter the greatest step of them all. 0061122416|5.0|Do not approach this story with the idea that Paulo Coelho is a master of prose. His simple style may turn off those more acquainted with bombastic, grandiose, or aggrandizing diction. Coelho's style can best be described as biblical. I personally love the simplistic, bold, and terse nature of the writing. If you cannot develop a taste for this style, at least understand the audience he was writing for. This book is, after all, has been translated into a huge number of languages worldwide. No doubt, a lot of Coelho's idiosyncrasies are lost in translation. Still, even after multiple reads it still manages to reach a level of near sublimity.<br /><br />There has been a bit of criticism here on the comments. To me, none of them really hold much water. The main one is above, but there are a few others. One of them is the \\"male-centric storyline\\". If you have even begun reading Coelho's books, you will see that half or more were written on and about a woman protagonist (Brida, Veronica Decides to Die, etc). More to the point, a larger focus on men is unavoidable - after all the main character is male. That does not make this book any less applicable to women. This book does not distinguish between men and women when it comes to its main messages and themes. The protagonist could have just as easily been a woman. It would not make any difference.<br /><br />The lack of character depth was also an obviously conscious decision on the part of Coelho to encourage the projecting of the reader onto the protagonist. Again, if you are familiar with Coelho's other work, you would know that he has at least some skill in developing his characters. The reader would be best treating this story less like the novel that it does not purport to be and more like a myth or fable.<br /><br />The argument that it \\"advocates materialism\\" makes little sense to me. I suppose if materialism means having a strong work ethic, being persistent, and pursuing one's dreams - then yes it does. The book does not say money is the root of all evil. Perhaps that is what the critics want it to say. If you are familiar with Coelho's works you would again know that his philosophy is that of enjoying life and the company of friends. Coelho's tone towards wealth is neutral and he even shows how easily it can be taken away (three times). He does not advocate strong attachment to money  - and certainly not materialism.<br /><br />This book is not for everyone, but I do know it is for most. It tells a universal message in a universal style. This message can be applied to all genders, ages, ethnicities, and creeds. Those looking for a book that can help them look smart would be best served looking elsewhere. This book revels in minimalism. Those looking for cynicism would best served looking elsewhere. You will only find an uplifting story here.root of all evil. Perhaps that is what the critics want it to say. B005HJWWW8|5.0|I recently purchased a portable BD/DVD player lacking any sort of tone controls on it (no bass-boost, bass, or treble).  All I had was volume control.  So what did I do?  I purchased a handy, little device called the FiiO-E6 headphone amplifier.  It comes with a long and a short connector cable.  You just use one of them for connecting the FiiO-E6 to your portable device's headphone jack.  Then you plug your headphones into the output jack of the FiiO-E6.  These things are generally advertised and promoted for use with iPods and the like.  However, I've always been more of a movie fan than a music person.  So I purchased it for use with my portable BD/DVD player--a Panasonic DMP-B200 to be exact.  Anyway, the FiiO-E6 has a nifty little EQ feature on it: Modes EQ1, EQ2, and EQ3.  EQ1 seems to boost bass and cut treble--handy for extremely bright movie soundtracks (or bright music); EQ2 (my favorite mode) boosts bass, but maintains the treble--personally I think I'm hearing a treble boost, as well; and EQ3 which seems to cut down on the volume (I hardly ever use EQ3).  EQ3 seems to have an intended purpose having to do with certain types of headphones which I have not researched closely.  Personally I use Sony MDR-V6 headphones which are fairly expensive studio monitors and which are full-size, around-the-ear, noise-blocking headphones of rather high quality.  So I really have no need to look into the EQ3 mode.  My Sony headphones work just fine with the FiiO-E6 in either EQ1 or EQ2.  At first I had my doubts about the FiiO-E6. But for the modest price I thought \\"why not give it a whirl.\\"  Sure I thought I would hear a muddying of the sound as opposed to just connecting my headphones directly to the portable BD/DVD player.  I thought I would hear hiss and all kinds of sound contamination with this thing.  But to my great surprise, there was no detectable audio contamination.  And yes, the FiiO-E6 actually improved the audio performance as was its intended purpose.  Itmade my DVD and Blu-ray watching experience totally pleasurable on my portable player.  This thing is great!  The FiiO-E6 has a built-in (apparently non-replaceable) rechargeable battery.  Each charge last for approximately 10 hours of audio performance (I have confirmed this).  Instead of recharging it via AC, you simply connect the supplied USB charging cable to any computer USB port and recharge it that way.  A red indicator light will go out once it is fully charged--roughly two or three hours (my guesstimate since I did not actually time it).  Of course, the battery is good for many hundreds of charges, but once exhausted you will simply have to purchase a new FiiO headphone amplifier product.  But the price is cheap and it won't break you.  This deivice is only about the size of a 50-cent piece and maybe just over 1/4 inch thick.  It has a clip for those who listen to music when jogging (I don't use the clip since I use my FiiO for movie watching).  Bottom line: If you have portable audio/video equimpment lacking tone controls, the FiiO-E6 is for you!  I didn't buy it for its volume control (I can control volume from my portable BD/DVD player).  Rather, I bought the FiiO-E6 for those handy EQ settings.  But yes, the FiiO-E6 does have a volume control as well.  When you're done using it, you must remember to shut the power on/off switch to \\"off\\" so that you do not drain the battery.  Likewise, if you're using the product, remember to turn it on first.  Otherwise you'll get no audio through your headphones (it does not pass through audio from your portable device unless it's actually on).  While these last two statements may seem like common sense, trust me; on a device this small and portable, forgetting to turn it on or off can be an easy mistake to make.  Anyway, I highly recommend the FiiO-E6 if you're looking for some tone control from your portable device as heard through headphones.

014312093X|5.0|There are two reasons I bothered to pick up this book.  Gary Oldman is starring in the new movie, whom I regard as one of the best actors to hit the silver screen,  and an interview with the director of the BBC TV series aired quite a few years ago, in which he said it is a thick story and it would be a good idea to read the book first so as not to get lost in the movie, so I took the advice.  I'm not a fan of cold-war spy stories,  and going in to it I didn't think I could really get into a genre who's day had past, being that the new enemy are the terrorists right?  Suffice it to say,  by 50 pages in, after the interrogation of Ricki Tarr,  I was completely hooked, and it got to the point I was giving up my usual routine to read, read, and read some more until I finished (I learned how to feed a 9-week-old baby a bottle while reading a book).  Yes the plot is intricate, it requires full attention, a good memory of many characters, motivations, and personal histories, but the pay-off is immensely rewarding.  It has all the low-burning suspense mixed with the right amount of white-knuckle moments (Peter Guillam brought in to face Percy, the final chapters' hunt and discovery of the mole) to make the story earthbound (i.e. believable - unlike a James Bond type tale).  The characters are complex, there are no gaping holes in the plot or discontinuity in the characters,  and a nice small number of sub-plots to break up the main story-line.  Let me emphasize the believability aspect... the lingo that's used,  the spy-craft described... they all make sense as Le Carre unfolds the character's actions and explains the reasoning deftly and subtly without turning the prose into textbook style.  Just some of the small details of what the people did, for example in a tense scene where a character is trying to sneak into a room in the Circus headquarters to photograph documents... the office is deathly quite although many people are in,  so the character doesn't sneak in, but just opens the door and shuts it behind him normally, because as Le Carre brilliantly lays out, a door slamming shut is less conspicuous in a quiet populated office than if it were very carefully opened (giving away that someone is trying to sneak in somewhere).  The book is filled with small details like that which make the read fun.  He also makes the characters very human, and mixes in a fair amount of mundane tasks and actions so that the story never feels like a dime-store hack spy novel.  It's moral and political sense is black and white sometimes, but gray most of the time as Le Carre takes the reader on a mystifying, twisting, illuminating journey into the world of British intelligence.  I don't think I'll be reading another cold-war spy novel anytime soon... but when I do it will have to measure up to this, and it won't be easy.  Maybe I'll pick up the others in Le Carre's trilogy in a few months after I've gotten up the nerve to devote another week to being lost in a book.  Buy the book, it won't disappoint unless you have a short attention span.  I really can't wait to see the movie now.

0374275637|5.0|Thinking Fast and Slow is a descriptive account of how the mind processes information and comes to conclusions.  Kahneman is a nobelaureate in economics, though his background is behavioural psychology.  He shows through carefully described experiments how our biases manifest themselves and convincingly shows that it is impossible for humans to process information rationally at all times.  The book is split into 5 sections - Two systems, a first description of the two kinds of thinking that guide us; Heuristics and Biases, a general overview of inherent biases and rules of thumbs we instinctively use incorrectly; Overconfidence, our ability to narrowly frame subjects at the expense of the worlds greater intricacy; Choices, how our choices are directly affected by framing, initial position and propensity for risk vs loss aversion; Two Selves, which is an overview of all that is covered.  I highly recommend reading the book to remind ourselves that we all have our biases and use associative reasoning by default and deductive reasoning only by forced effort.  The author starts the book by describing a general framework for thinking about how we process information.  In particular the author describes our thought process as being defined by two systems - System 1 is our instinctual thought process.  It processes information subconsiously and with high speed.  System 2 is our conscious slower and more deliberate mind.  It is able to deduce and calculate for us and can reason assumptions to form conclusions.  It takes more effort and takes much longer to process information with this system.  With this framework, the author asks the reader a series of questions and asks for the honest and instinctual answers to show how and where System 1 is error prone.  We are all a different mix of deliberate as well as instinctual thinking and throughout the course of the book I have no doubt that every reader will be caught by at least one heuristic mistake.  The result of this interactive form of writing is a deeper appreciation of our individual intrinsic biases (as I assume each reader is biased by various factors in differing magnitudes).  After the framework for analysis is developed the author's core of the book are chapters on the kinds of \\"mistakes\\" we make.  The first is on the heuristics we employ and the biases we can get influenced by, to give a flavor he discusses, overconfidence in small sample pools, anchoring and mean reversion among others.  The author then tackles overconfidence and how simple rules tend to be more predictive than complicated narrative based predictions.  The author introduces basic ideas from Bayesian probability to show how we misuse conditional expectation.  He discusses how it is hard to take a step back from yourself and try to take an outsiders view of your actions vs just focusing on the natural insiders view that comes more naturally.  The author finally tackles a strong pillar of modern economics, utility theory.  He shows how utility often fails to describe our behaviour as our actual utility is a function of our initial endowment.  In particular our decisions match utilitarian conclusions when we are deciding between two positive outcomes, but is the opposite often between to bad outcomes.  Our desire to gamble when we have something to lose is an intuitive but irrational response and shown to be a function of framing and endowment.  This kind of contradictory behaviour is studied with carefully reasoned examples.  The last major topic the author discusses is memory and how we have two selves.  If people watch ted.com this part of the book is very similar to the author's talk.  In particular the author discusses how the experiencing self has occasionally diverging interests from the remembering self.  To give more background, the author introduces the idea that when one remembers something, the end has a very strong impact; in particular the author believes that when remembering the experience is predominantly an average of two variables, the average aggregate experience and the end.  The result of this is a short great experience that ends well is more valuable to the remembering self than a long great experience that has a bad ending (despite the experiencing self getting more positive moments out of the latter than the former).  The author leaves the conclusions of this up to the reader to think about.  Thinking Fast and Slow is a great guide to behavioural economics.  Much of the book has similar material in behavioural finance books published over the last decade, but Kahneman (one of the pioneers in the field) puts it together in a complete way.  It is hard to come through at the end without at least having learned something about oneself.  The book definitely has examples which most readers would question whether the conclusion draws naturally from the example.  I know from my perspective some of the side by side experiments who were supposed to be equivalent were very much not in my opinion (ie an experiment asking how much you would pay for an antidote to a probably deadly virus vs how much would you expect to recieve to join a probably deadly science experiment- with those numbers being different implying we arent comparing the situations rationally).  But this is part of why its so interesting as how one qualifies ones own answers sheds light on our own thought process etc.  I highly recommend reading as by the end it is hard not to see how we fall into many a mental trap and more importantly one is slightly better able to see yourself from outside yourself.

B00555X8OA|5.0|I was really impressed with Kahneman's book. I had already been familiar with Kahneman and Tversky's work in prospect theory and Richard Thaler's work. But there was so much here that I had not known about before.  This is the best book I have read in the last year and the only book I plan to reread.  Kahneman also covers the basics of behavioral economics (such as anchoring) very clearly. He describes how the experiments were done and in many cases you can walk through them yourselves and see how you would react.  One of the results I found most disturbing was the account of how we mis-remember pain. (This is fairly late in the book, you won't see it in the Kindle sample.)  In many cases we remember painful experiences as being less onerous than they were. This means a physician doing something like removing bandages or performing a colonoscopy may have to choose between what will give the patient the worst experience as he is experiencing it now, or the worst memories of it later. When ordinary subjects are polled, they usually choose the thing that would give them the least bad memories, which often means choosing to suffer more in real time. Nobody, I think, knows what is really the best approach in these cases.

B005DKLPF6|4.0|Mastodon have lasted a few years now and have a recorded output large enough now that's allowed fans to have divisive opinions. There are those who prefer the earlier brazen power of when they were a standard bearer for the hottest new genre in metal music. There's some who really get behind the conceptual aspirations of their most famous albums and others who could give less of a damn about metal altogether and fell head over shoulders for the progressive ambitions of2009's Crack the Skye. Also known as the album that brought them to an even bigger audience and split opinion even further. Were Mastodon becoming overly pretentious, luring in new audiences and indulging lofty ideas at the expense of crafting memorable riffs?<br /><br />Personally speaking I've liked every undertaking of theirs, at the risk of sounding like an apologist for the band, for which I'm certainly not. I recognize that by now a band of this caliber should have had a misstep or two. What's curious about their latest effort is that it almost sounds pre-calculated to satiate the group of fans who didn't go crazy for \\"Skye\\" yet this doesn't sound like a forced set of songs at all. The songs follow more rigid structures than ever before and still retain all the things that make this band any good in the first place. That is, impeccable dual guitar riffing that plays on the very best classical thrash templates, drumming that's tight as a vacuum seal and generally memorable lyrical themes. What's hard to deny is the increased accessibility with the repeated chorus refrains and emphasized vocals. It's interesting that \\"Skye\\" was their most successful album to date but featured their longest and most progressive songs. Is it that they're no longer interested in continuing that style or is this batch of songs, free of overarching concepts and dare I say, even a compelling flow, more pleasing to play as both musicians and to a crowd that at the end of the day they want to unify as much as humanly possible?<br /><br />In short, the music here kicks ass, plain and simple. There's absolutely nothing here as cerebral or exciting as their best works, but after many listens I've not once gone into this album approaching it it's the very best Mastodon can do. On that front, they've succeeded wildly in presenting a good number of songs that showcase just about everything they can do without expectations weighing them down.hort, the music here kicks ass, plain and simple. 1844256383|5.0|By way of background: I have played the saxophone for 32 years, and I earned a bachelor's degree in music with sax as my instrument of choice.  Having gone in another direction (science) professionally for the day job, I am a skilled \\"weekend warrior\\" who earns thousands a year playing the majority of Saturday evenings, but do not consider myself anything like a recording artist.  Despite a long history of playing the sax, I have always just taken it to my tech when anything but the slightest issue went wrong.  Therefore, I had always wanted to learn more about the \\"guts\\" of the sax and its mechanistic operation, if nothing else to save a few bucks (or should I say \\"quid\\"?) on more mundane fixes or even diagnostics if something is clanking or some notes sound stuffy.  Select portions of this book, especially the parts about lubrication (such as the very best oil to use) and checking for and tweaking regulation of the keywork-- nails it for me!    This book is clearly written to span the entire range of expertise with the saxophone, from the prospective first-time buyer of a sax, to the technically proficient handyman that is on the verge of giving a go to stripping and rebuilding a sax (that does not have any mechanical/structural damage).  Therefore, the first two or three chapters were of little use to me, but so what?  At $23 or so from this site, the \\"sweet spot\\" chapters that meet me where I'm at as a handy-man (not terribly proficient) and the final chapters with guidance on more ambitious interventions should I get brave down the road) make it a worthwhile purchase.  In sum, the book has great, to-the-point, myth-busting writing, excellent studio-quality photography, and ample diagrams of key concepts and parts.  This manual IMO has something to offer pretty much any player who is not already comprehensively repairing his or her own axes.  It will meet every one else where they are.  Previous reviewers who knock this tome for being too rudimentary miss its intention IMO, and are probably at the expertise level where they should be obtaining materials and training in full-blown sax tech.    Despite how it is likely that moderately experienced players may not need much of the introductory material, and how it is likely that many players like me will not go so far as to replace and seat pads, the value of the comprehensive span of material for the price, coupled with high production quality of the book earns it five stars.  I think the maintenance chapters alone make it worth purchase by parents of students who are really giving the sax a go past the initial trial period of a rental.

1451648537|2.0|The first half of this book is fairly interesting and well constructed, even if it gets some facts wrong, glosses over some very damning things (like just how bad the first Apple factory was), fails to question some of Jobs' assertions and could have been trimmed by 20%. Nevertheless, you get a good feel for the man.  The last half of the book is terrible. It feels rushed and poorly edited, with sections awkwardly stuck in with no sense of the timeline or context. At one point, I suddenly realized the book had gone back several years with only an ambiguous reference. At the two thirds mark, it became such an amateurish hack job that I had a hard time not tossing the book aside.  While other reviewers have complained about the references to the \\"reality distortion field\\" it was a critical part of understanding Jobs. I'd been hearing about it since the late 80s and in much more negative and cruel terms than the author references. Unfortunately, Isaacson seems to have gotten caught up in it. Once Jobs returns to Apple, Isaacson starts ceasing to be both objective and informative. I suspect that this is due, in part, that those that could give an objective counter view, such as Wozniak, Kawasaki and Hertzfeld, simply aren't present or Isaacson didn't bother talking to the strong personalities who were present. The result is increasingly a hagiography.  One thing that really disturbed me are the times Isaacson accepts at face value Jobs claims to have created an idea or invention even though it was known to have been invented by someone else. It is worrisome because Isaacson documents several instances when the actual provenance of an idea or invention is shown, yet he fails to follow through with some rather outlandish claims. This would have been valuable to both help set the record straight, but to also fully understand Jobs influence on these ideas and inventions, both positive and negative.

I advise the prospective buyer to buy a nice set of headphones (Grado, etc) and a separate mic. I hear throat mics are cheap these days. First and foremost is the fact that so many of our \\"best and brightest\\" ivy league grads have internalized no moral compass other than the value of making as much money as possible. This is ultimately a failure of our educational system, which has concluded that values education is off limits, and of our culture as a whole, which thinks that all values are relative and therefore none may be taught. When you have your best and brightest singularly focused on coming up with as many ways as possible to make money with no checks on their activities, you have a recipe for disaster. Government regulators and ratings agencies should have served as countervailing checks.  But the ratings agencies were all infected by a conflict of interest -- if they downgraded any financial instrument the seller would stop paying their fees -- and because they paid their employees so little, there was no talent in these agencies to ferret out what the \\"best and brightest\\" were doing even if there hadn't been a conflict of interest that would have suppressed any such knowledge. Since what brought the system down was a system of regularized betting on the market taking the form of \\"insurance,\\" the government should have strictly regulated this the way other insurance is regulated. Because government employees are either lifers with little talent or revolving-door denizens who know they will need a job when they return to the private sector, government employees either didn't have the smarts to figure out what was going on or wouldn't tell because of the future job conflict of interest. Add on top of this the relentless conservative ideology propaganda wantonly promoted by the people making billions off this scheme as well as by their media stooges that \\"the market\\" somehow itself \\"knows best,\\" and that any government interference would spook it. The coup de grace was the unthinking use ofmathematical formulas by people who should have known better, e.g., formulas relating to \\"diversification\\" of risk that allowed B-rated investments to be transformed from dross into triple A-rated gold by the mere application of a formula. Garbage in, garbage out. My one quibble with this excellent volume is that the synthetic CDO concept is not adequately explained. My sense is that the biggest part of this problem was the fact that Goldman Sachs, et al. were able to create entirely theoretical security instruments out of thin air:  the synthetic CDOs, as Lewis says, were \\"a near-perfect replica of a subprime mortgage bond\\" but with \\"no actual home loans or home buyers.\\" (Pp. 75-76).  I think this is the crux of the matter but it needs a better explainer. But overall, a great read.

B004DR9OGC|4.0|Anne Bronte's The Tenant of Wildfell Hall was bold for its time, tackling taboo topics such as domestic violence and female emancipation. Not as well known as her other two sisters, Emily (who wrote Wuthering Heights), and Charlotte (who wrote Jane Eyre), Anne's novel is nevertheless an important work, and well worth a read.<br /><br />This 1996 BBC adaptation stars Tara Fitzgerald as Helen Graham, an attractive woman who appears to be very secretive, keeping quietly to herself when she arrives in the guise of a widow at Wildfell Hall, in 19th century Yorkshire. Together with her young son, Helen attempts to maintain a low profile, a futile effort as her very presence arouses the locals' suspicions, resulting in malicious gossip. It is only at the insistence of a local gentleman farmer, Gilbert Markham (Toby Stephens), that Helen reveals the agonizing secrets of her past and the life of corruption that she fled for her sake, as well as her young son's.<br /><br />The production qualities are quite well done, with beautiful shots of the picturesque English countryside, and though the soundtrack isn't exactly inspiring, the talented cast more than makes up for any deficiencies in the production. This is well worth a watch for those who are fans of well-made period dramas, and a must-watch for fans of the Bronte sisters' works.

B000JMKQX0|5.0|World War Z / 9780307346612<br /><br />This book is a collection of 'interviews' and vignettes in the wake of the world wide zombie war. I'd had some concerns going in that the book wouldn't sustain suspense very well, since all the stories are told retroactively (so you know the teller survived their tale), but I shouldn't have been worried. \\"World War Z\\" brings suspense to the table, along with an incredible freshness of tone and ideas.<br /><br />The new ideas are where this book really shines. Brooks doesn't just reach for the low-hanging zombie fruit; he looks at the zombie apocalypse from a variety of lenses. Some of the new ideas explored here include: The spreading of the virus via the organ transplant black market. Neighborhood cleanup in the wake of the war and what that would entail. Feral children and animals and the rise of both after a global apocalypse. The dangers of the \\"Lone Rangers\\", particularly ones who set bombs willy-nilly. A look at both successful and unsuccessful military initiatives. Life aboard a submarine as an escape from the walking dead. Tracking zombie movements along coastlines via tagging. And those are all things off the top of my head -- there's a huge wealth of ideas in this book that I haven't even touched on.<br /><br />There's so many things to like about this book, but I love Brooks' treatment of minorities. This isn't your average zombie apocalypse where only the meaty manly men survive. We get to see a female fighter pilot trek to a pick-up point when her cargo explodes and she has to eject from the plane. We see a man in a wheelchair and hear him talk about his time on the neighborhood patrol. We talk to a blind man who lived in the wilds of Japan on his own, listening carefully for the zombies and killing any who came within his range. It's so rare to see such a wide range of people in a zombie novel, and Brooks delivers in a way that a more focused novel simply couldn't. I also love that the novel explores the apocalypse from a very solid class perspective -- the rich who treat the whole thing as a bizarre reality show fare (on the whole) much worse than the average folks on the street.<br /><br />I read \\"World War Z\\" for a book club, but it's one of the few books that I finished only to want to pick it up and read it again. I recommend the book highly, though I will note one pet peeve: Brooks' interviews are written in the vernacular and I did get very tired seeing the word \\"crazy\\" thrown around when really what was meant was \\"silly\\", \\"foolish\\", \\"ill-advised\\", or some other, better word. Still, if you can get past that, I think you'll like this novel if you have any affinity for zombie literature.<br /><br />~ Ana Mardollspective -- the rich who treat the whole thing as a bizarre reality show fare (on the whole) much worse than the average folks on the street. B005LAJ1H2|2.0|Boardwalk Empire had an interesting premise and some good historical material to mine. It worked ok for the first season. But as the second season went on, it became clear that the premise had been played out. The setting and the characters had been used up. And there was nowhere to go but down.  The first major problem was that the show degenerated into \\"Good Irish Wife Playhouse\\". This is a gangster drama. Most people (including me) want to see gangster drama rather than the overblown domestic trials and tribulations of I dont know how many \\"good\\" Irish wives in the cast. And because its Good Irish Wife playhouse, we have to treat the death of her wifebeating, baby-killing drunken husband Hans as great crime. Somehow nobody makes the point that killing a guy who beats his pregnant wife up in they way he did can be seen more as a sort of rough justice rather than a moral crime. I'm not sure anyone even bothers to make the point about what sort of guy Hans was.  About halfway through the season, they seemed to realize that the plot had run out. So they started killing and/or wrecking every character in the cast. By the end of the season, almost everyone but Thompson (and wife) was dead. Every episode seemed to require a shocking death or a shocking betrayal that often made little sense and were worse yet, against the actual history.  The real Commodore lived to 1934. Yet in the show he is killed in the early 1920s to provide more mixed up family drama leading to the death of another character. The Commodore was an important character to the show because he provided the bridge between the political machine and the business community.  The show also fails to understand the rules of a 1920s political machine. Gangsters were not going to openly kill high-profile businessmen or senior politicians without consequences. Especially a low-level gangster in Atlantic City. The show also doesn't understand that the local political machine fits into a bigger state-level machinewith its own priorities.   The show also ignores the democratic party in terms of how things got done in that era. The democratic party (who controlled the statehouse at the time) would have been leading the charge to get members of the atlantic city machine. And the democratic machine would have existed in parallel with the republican machine. Working with gangsters in New York or other east coast cities would require political negotiations as well as negotiations with gangsters. Disputes between gangsters turn into disputes between politicians. The writers just don't understand and don't care much about how the era really worked.   By the end of the second season, everyone is dead and the show seems to have nowhere left to go. Its been renewed for a third season. But what that will consist of is unclear. They can either abandon history or they can repeat themselves.   Steve Buscemi does an outstanding job in the lead role. He is the only person associated with this program who deserves awards. Its just a pity that the writers are not up to creating better material for him to play.
